<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Faraday" redirects here. <p><b>Michael Faraday</b> <span class="noexcerpt nowraplinks" style="font-size:85%; font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a></span> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/ær/: 'arr' in 'marry'">ær</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>; 22 September 1791 – 25 August 1867) was an English <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a> who contributed to the study of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electrochemistry" title="Electrochemistry">electrochemistry</a>. His main discoveries include the principles underlying <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_induction" title="Electromagnetic induction">electromagnetic induction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diamagnetism" title="Diamagnetism">diamagnetism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a>.
</p><p>Although Faraday received little formal education, he was one of the most influential scientists in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rao_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rao-1">[1]</a></sup> It was by his research on the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">magnetic field</a> around a <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">conductor</a> carrying a <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">direct current</a> that Faraday established the basis for the concept of the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">electromagnetic field</a> in physics. Faraday also established that <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetism</a> could affect <a href="/wiki/Ray_(optics)" title="Ray (optics)">rays of light</a> and that there was an underlying relationship between the two phenomena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncBrit_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncBrit-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEUK_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEEUK-3">[3]</a></sup> He similarly discovered the principles of electromagnetic induction, diamagnetism, and the <a href="/wiki/Faraday%27s_laws_of_electrolysis" title="Faraday's laws of electrolysis">laws of electrolysis</a>. His <a href="/wiki/Invention" title="Invention">inventions</a> of <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electromagnetic rotary devices</a> formed the foundation of electric motor technology, and it was largely due to his efforts that <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> became practical for use in technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Field_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Field-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a chemist, Faraday discovered <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a>, investigated the <a href="/wiki/Clathrate_hydrate" title="Clathrate hydrate">clathrate hydrate</a> of chlorine, invented an early form of the <a href="/wiki/Bunsen_burner" title="Bunsen burner">Bunsen burner</a> and the system of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation_number" title="Oxidation number">oxidation numbers</a>, and popularised terminology such as "<a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">anode</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">cathode</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Electrode" title="Electrode">electrode</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ion</a>". Faraday ultimately became the first and foremost <a href="/wiki/Fullerian_Professor_of_Chemistry" title="Fullerian Professor of Chemistry">Fullerian Professor of Chemistry</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution" title="Royal Institution">Royal Institution</a>, a lifetime position.
</p><p>Faraday was an excellent experimentalist who conveyed his ideas in clear and simple language; his mathematical abilities, however, did not extend as far as <a href="/wiki/Trigonometry" title="Trigonometry">trigonometry</a> and were limited to the simplest algebra. <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> took the work of Faraday and others and summarized it in a set of equations which is accepted as the basis of all modern theories of electromagnetic phenomena. On Faraday's uses of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lines_of_force" title="Lines of force">lines of force</a>, Maxwell wrote that they show Faraday "to have been in reality a mathematician of a very high order – one from whom the mathematicians of the future may derive valuable and fertile methods."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaxwellSciPapVol1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-MaxwellSciPapVol1-5">[5]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">SI</a> unit of <a href="/wiki/Capacitance" title="Capacitance">capacitance</a> is named in his honour: the <a href="/wiki/Farad" title="Farad">farad</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> kept a picture of Faraday on his study wall, alongside pictures of <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Arthur Schopenhauer</a> and James Clerk Maxwell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Physicist <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> stated, "When we consider the magnitude and extent of his discoveries and their influence on the progress of science and of industry, there is no honour too great to pay to the memory of Faraday, one of the greatest scientific discoverers of all time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rao_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rao-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Scientific_achievements"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Scientific achievements</span></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Faraday&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Faraday&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Michael Faraday was born on 22 September 1791 in <a href="/wiki/Newington_Butts" title="Newington Butts">Newington Butts</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">Surrey</a> (which is now part of the <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Southwark" title="London Borough of Southwark">London Borough of Southwark</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>  His family was not well off. His father, James, was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Glasite" title="Glasite">Glasite</a> sect of Christianity. James Faraday moved his wife, Margaret (née Hastwell),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> and two children to London during the winter of 1790 from <a href="/wiki/Outhgill" title="Outhgill">Outhgill</a> in <a href="/wiki/Westmorland" title="Westmorland">Westmorland</a>, where he had been an apprentice to the village blacksmith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>  Michael was born in the autumn of that year. The young Michael Faraday, who was the third of four children, having only the most basic school education, had to <a href="/wiki/Autodidacticism" title="Autodidacticism">educate himself</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the age of 14 he became an apprentice to <a href="/wiki/George_Riebau" title="George Riebau">George Riebau</a>, a local bookbinder and bookseller in Blandford Street.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> During his seven-year apprenticeship Faraday read many books, including <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Watts" title="Isaac Watts">Isaac Watts</a>'s <i>The Improvement of the Mind</i>, and he enthusiastically implemented the principles and suggestions contained therein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> He also developed an interest in science, especially in electricity. Faraday was particularly inspired by the book <i>Conversations on Chemistry</i> by <a href="/wiki/Jane_Marcet" title="Jane Marcet">Jane Marcet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adult_life">Adult life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Faraday&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Adult life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1437" data-file-width="1110" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg/170px-M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg/255px-M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg/340px-M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M_Faraday_Th_Phillips_oil_1842.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Faraday in 1842 by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Phillips" title="Thomas Phillips">Thomas Phillips</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1812, at the age of 20 and at the end of his apprenticeship, Faraday attended lectures by the eminent English chemist <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution" title="Royal Institution">Royal Institution</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Tatum_(scientist)" title="John Tatum (scientist)">John Tatum</a>, founder of the City Philosophical Society. Many of the tickets for these lectures were given to Faraday by <a href="/wiki/William_Dance" title="William Dance">William Dance</a>, who was one of the founders of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Philharmonic_Society" title="Royal Philharmonic Society">Royal Philharmonic Society</a>. Faraday subsequently sent Davy a 300-page book based on notes that he had taken during these lectures. Davy's reply was immediate, kind, and favourable. In 1813, when Davy damaged his eyesight in an accident with <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_trichloride" title="Nitrogen trichloride">nitrogen trichloride</a>, he decided to employ Faraday as an assistant. Coincidentally one of the Royal Institution's assistants, John Payne, was sacked and Sir Humphry Davy had been asked to find a replacement; thus he appointed Faraday as Chemical Assistant at the Royal Institution on 1 March 1813.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncBrit_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-EncBrit-2">[2]</a></sup> Very soon Davy entrusted Faraday with the preparation of nitrogen trichloride samples, and they both were injured in an explosion of this very sensitive substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Faraday married Sarah Barnard (1800–1879) on 12 June 1821.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> They met through their families at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sandemanian" title="Sandemanian">Sandemanian</a> church, and he confessed his faith to the Sandemanian congregation the month after they were married. They had no children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Faraday was a devout Christian; his Sandemanian denomination was an offshoot of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a>. Well after his marriage, he served as <a href="/wiki/Deacon" title="Deacon">deacon</a> and for two terms as an <a href="/wiki/Elder_(Christianity)" title="Elder (Christianity)">elder</a> in the meeting house of his youth. His church was located at Paul's Alley in the <a href="/wiki/Barbican_Estate" title="Barbican Estate">Barbican</a>. This meeting house relocated in 1862 to <a href="/wiki/Barnsbury" title="Barnsbury">Barnsbury</a> Grove, <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Islington" title="London Borough of Islington">Islington</a>; this North London location was where Faraday served the final two years of his second term as elder prior to his resignation from that post.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>  Biographers have noted that "a strong sense of the unity of God and nature pervaded Faraday's life and work."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life">Later life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Faraday&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Later life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_offering_the_presidency_o_Wellcome_L0022806.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1196" data-file-width="1580" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Three_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_offering_the_presidency_o_Wellcome_L0022806.jpg/220px-Three_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_offering_the_presidency_o_Wellcome_L0022806.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Three_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_offering_the_presidency_o_Wellcome_L0022806.jpg/330px-Three_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_offering_the_presidency_o_Wellcome_L0022806.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Three_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_offering_the_presidency_o_Wellcome_L0022806.jpg/440px-Three_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_offering_the_presidency_o_Wellcome_L0022806.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_offering_the_presidency_o_Wellcome_L0022806.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Three Fellows of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> offering the presidency to Faraday, 1857</div></div></div>
<p>In June 1832, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a> granted Faraday an honorary <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Civil_Law" title="Doctor of Civil Law">Doctor of Civil Law</a> degree. During his lifetime, he was offered a <a href="/wiki/Orders,_decorations,_and_medals_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Orders, decorations, and medals of the United Kingdom">knighthood</a> in recognition for his services to science, which he <a href="/wiki/List_of_people_who_have_declined_a_British_honour" title="List of people who have declined a British honour">turned down</a> on religious grounds, believing that it was against the word of the Bible to accumulate riches and pursue worldly reward, and stating that he preferred to remain "plain Mr Faraday to the end".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Elected a <a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">Fellow</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> in 1824, he twice refused to become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President_of_the_Royal_Society" title="President of the Royal Society">President</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> He became the first <a href="/wiki/Fullerian_Professor_of_Chemistry" title="Fullerian Professor of Chemistry">Fullerian Professor of Chemistry</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution" title="Royal Institution">Royal Institution</a> in 1833.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1832, Faraday was elected a Foreign Honorary Member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAAS_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAAS-24">[24]</a></sup> He was elected a foreign member of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Swedish_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences">Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences</a> in 1838. In 1840, he was elected to the <a href="/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society" title="American Philosophical Society">American Philosophical Society</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> He was one of eight foreign members elected to the <a href="/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences" title="French Academy of Sciences">French Academy of Sciences</a> in 1844.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In 1849 he was elected as associated member to the Royal Institute of the Netherlands, which two years later became the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Netherlands_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Royal Netherlands Academy of Arts and Sciences">Royal Netherlands Academy of Arts and Sciences</a> and he was subsequently made foreign member.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_Michael_grave.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1772" data-file-width="1188" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Faraday_Michael_grave.jpg/170px-Faraday_Michael_grave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Faraday_Michael_grave.jpg/255px-Faraday_Michael_grave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Faraday_Michael_grave.jpg/340px-Faraday_Michael_grave.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_Michael_grave.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Faraday's grave at <a href="/wiki/Highgate_Cemetery" title="Highgate Cemetery">Highgate Cemetery</a>, London</div></div></div>
<p>Faraday suffered a nervous breakdown in 1839 but eventually returned to his investigations into electromagnetism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In 1848, as a result of representations by the <a href="/wiki/Albert,_Prince_Consort" title="Albert, Prince Consort">Prince Consort</a>, Faraday was awarded a <a href="/wiki/Grace_and_favour" title="Grace and favour">grace and favour</a> house in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hampton_Court" title="Hampton Court">Hampton Court</a> in Middlesex, free of all expenses and upkeep. This was the Master Mason's House, later called Faraday House, and now No. 37 Hampton Court Road. In 1858 Faraday retired to live there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Having provided a number of various service projects for the British government, when asked by the government to advise on the production of chemical weapons for use in the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> (1853–1856), Faraday refused to participate, citing ethical reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croddy_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Croddy-30">[30]</a></sup><span class="anchor" id="death"></span>
</p><p>Faraday died at his house at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hampton_Court" title="Hampton Court">Hampton Court</a> on 25 August 1867, aged 75.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> He had some years before turned down an offer of burial in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> upon his death, but he has a memorial plaque there, near <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>'s tomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Faraday was interred in the <a href="/wiki/Dissenter" title="Dissenter">dissenters</a>' (non-<a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglican</a>) section of <a href="/wiki/Highgate_Cemetery" title="Highgate Cemetery">Highgate Cemetery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_achievements">Scientific achievements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Faraday&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Scientific achievements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Faraday&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ri_2014_-_glass_making_-_Faraday.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5184" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ri_2014_-_glass_making_-_Faraday.jpg/180px-Ri_2014_-_glass_making_-_Faraday.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ri_2014_-_glass_making_-_Faraday.jpg/270px-Ri_2014_-_glass_making_-_Faraday.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ri_2014_-_glass_making_-_Faraday.jpg/360px-Ri_2014_-_glass_making_-_Faraday.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ri_2014_-_glass_making_-_Faraday.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Equipment used by Faraday to make glass on display at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution" title="Royal Institution">Royal Institution</a> in London</div></div></div>
<p>Faraday's earliest chemical work was as an assistant to <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a>. Faraday was specifically involved in the study of <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>; he discovered two new compounds of chlorine and <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>. He also conducted the first rough experiments on the diffusion of gases, a phenomenon that was first pointed out by <a href="/wiki/John_Dalton" title="John Dalton">John Dalton</a>. The physical importance of this phenomenon was more fully revealed by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Graham_(chemist)" title="Thomas Graham (chemist)">Thomas Graham</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Loschmidt" title="Joseph Loschmidt">Joseph Loschmidt</a>. Faraday succeeded in liquefying several gases, investigated the alloys of steel, and produced several new kinds of glass intended for optical purposes. A specimen of one of these heavy glasses subsequently became historically important; when the glass was placed in a magnetic field Faraday determined the rotation of the plane of polarisation of light. This specimen was also the first substance found to be repelled by the poles of a magnet.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Faraday invented an early form of what was to become the <a href="/wiki/Bunsen_burner" title="Bunsen burner">Bunsen burner</a>, which is in practical use in science laboratories around the world as a convenient source of heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
Faraday worked extensively in the field of chemistry, discovering chemical substances such as <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a> (which he called bicarburet of hydrogen) and liquefying gases such as chlorine. The liquefying of gases helped to establish that gases are the vapours of liquids possessing a very low boiling point and gave a more solid basis to the concept of molecular aggregation. In 1820 Faraday reported the first synthesis of compounds made from carbon and chlorine, <a href="/wiki/Hexachloroethane" title="Hexachloroethane">C<sub>2</sub>Cl<sub>6</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/Tetrachloroethylene" title="Tetrachloroethylene">C<sub>2</sub>Cl<sub>4</sub></a>, and published his results the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Faraday also determined the composition of the chlorine <a href="/wiki/Clathrate_hydrate" title="Clathrate hydrate">clathrate hydrate</a>, which had been discovered by Humphry Davy in 1810.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Faraday is also responsible for discovering the <a href="/wiki/Faraday%27s_laws_of_electrolysis" title="Faraday's laws of electrolysis">laws of electrolysis</a>, and for popularizing terminology such as <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">anode</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">cathode</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrode" title="Electrode">electrode</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ion</a>, terms proposed in large part by <a href="/wiki/William_Whewell" title="William Whewell">William Whewell</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Faraday was the first to report what later came to be called metallic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanoparticles" title="Nanoparticles">nanoparticles</a>. In 1847 he discovered that the optical properties of gold <a href="/wiki/Colloid" title="Colloid">colloids</a> differed from those of the corresponding bulk metal. This was probably the first reported observation of the effects of <a href="/wiki/Quantum" title="Quantum">quantum</a> size, and might be considered to be the birth of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanoscience" title="Nanoscience">nanoscience</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electricity_and_magnetism">Electricity and magnetism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Faraday&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Electricity and magnetism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Faraday is best known for his work regarding electricity and magnetism. His first recorded experiment was the construction of a <a href="/wiki/Voltaic_pile" title="Voltaic pile">voltaic pile</a> with seven <a href="/wiki/Halfpenny_(British_pre-decimal_coin)" title="Halfpenny (British pre-decimal coin)">British halfpenny</a> coins, stacked together with seven discs of sheet zinc, and six pieces of paper moistened with salt water. With this pile he decomposed <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_sulfate" title="Magnesium sulfate">sulfate of magnesia</a> (first letter to Abbott, 12 July 1812).
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="545" data-file-width="538" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg/170px-Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg/255px-Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg/340px-Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Electromagnetic rotation experiment of Faraday, 1821,  the first demonstration of the conversion of electrical energy into motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 1821, soon after the Danish physicist and chemist <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted" title="Hans Christian Ørsted">Hans Christian Ørsted</a> discovered the phenomenon of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetism</a>, Davy and <a href="/wiki/William_Hyde_Wollaston" title="William Hyde Wollaston">William Hyde Wollaston</a> tried, but failed, to design an <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEUK_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-IEEUK-3">[3]</a></sup> Faraday, having discussed the problem with the two men, went on to build two devices to produce what he called "electromagnetic rotations".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> One of these, now known as the <a href="/wiki/Homopolar_motor" title="Homopolar motor">homopolar motor</a>, caused a continuous circular motion that was engendered by the circular magnetic force around a wire that extended into a pool of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> wherein was placed a magnet; the wire would then rotate around the magnet if supplied with current from a chemical battery. These experiments and inventions, first carried out in the basement of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution" title="Royal Institution">Royal Institution</a> on 3 September 1821, formed the foundation of modern electromagnetic technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Electric_Motion_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Electric_Motion-45">[45]</a></sup> Faraday published the results of his discovery in the <i><a href="/wiki/Quarterly_Journal_of_Science" title="Quarterly Journal of Science">Quarterly Journal of Science</a></i>, and sent copies of his paper along with pocket-sized models of his device to colleagues around the world so they could also witness the phenomenon of electromagnetic rotations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Electric_Motion_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Electric_Motion-45">[45]</a></sup> In his excitement, Faraday hastily released the paper which had not acknowledged his work with either Wollaston or Davy. The resulting controversy within the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> strained his mentor relationship with Davy and may well have contributed to Faraday's assignment to other activities, which consequently prevented his involvement in electromagnetic research for several years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Induction_experiment.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="826" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Induction_experiment.png/220px-Induction_experiment.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Induction_experiment.png/330px-Induction_experiment.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Induction_experiment.png/440px-Induction_experiment.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Induction_experiment.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of Faraday's 1831 experiments demonstrating induction. The liquid battery <i>(right)</i> sends an electric current through the small coil <i>(A)</i>.  When it is moved in or out of the large coil <i>(B)</i>, its magnetic field induces a momentary voltage in the coil, which is detected by the galvanometer <i>(G)</i>.</div></div></div>
<p>From his initial discovery in 1821, Faraday continued his laboratory work, exploring electromagnetic properties of materials and developing requisite experience. In 1824, Faraday briefly set up a circuit to study whether a magnetic field could regulate the flow of a current in an adjacent wire, but he found no such relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> This experiment followed similar work conducted with light and magnets three years earlier that yielded identical results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> During the next seven years, Faraday spent much of his time perfecting his recipe for optical quality (heavy) glass, borosilicate of lead,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> which he used in his future studies connecting light with magnetism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>  In his spare time, Faraday continued publishing his experimental work on optics and electromagnetism; he conducted correspondence with scientists whom he had met on his journeys across Europe with Davy, and who were also working on electromagnetism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Two years after the death of Davy, in 1831, he began his great series of experiments in which he discovered <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_induction" title="Electromagnetic induction">electromagnetic induction</a>, recording in his laboratory diary on 28 October 1831 he was; "making many experiments with the great magnet of the Royal Society".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_emf_experiment.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="365" data-file-width="1035" decoding="async" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Faraday_emf_experiment.svg/220px-Faraday_emf_experiment.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Faraday_emf_experiment.svg/330px-Faraday_emf_experiment.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Faraday_emf_experiment.svg/440px-Faraday_emf_experiment.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_emf_experiment.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A diagram of Faraday's iron ring-coil apparatus</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_disk_generator.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Faraday_disk_generator.jpg/170px-Faraday_disk_generator.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Faraday_disk_generator.jpg/255px-Faraday_disk_generator.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Faraday_disk_generator.jpg/340px-Faraday_disk_generator.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_disk_generator.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Built in 1831, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faraday_disc" title="Faraday disc">Faraday disc</a> was the first <a href="/wiki/Electric_generator" title="Electric generator">electric generator</a>. The horseshoe-shaped magnet <i>(A)</i> created a magnetic field through the disc <i>(D)</i>. When the disc was turned, this induced an electric current radially outward from the centre toward the rim.  The current flowed out through the sliding spring contact <i>m</i>, through the external circuit, and back into the centre of the disc through the axle.</div></div></div>
<p>Faraday's breakthrough came when he wrapped two insulated coils of wire around an iron ring, and found that, upon passing a current through one coil, a momentary current was induced in the other coil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEUK_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-IEEUK-3">[3]</a></sup> This phenomenon is now known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutual_induction" title="Mutual induction">mutual induction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The iron ring-coil apparatus is still on display at the Royal Institution. In subsequent experiments, he found that if he moved a magnet through a loop of wire an electric current flowed in that wire. The current also flowed if the loop was moved over a stationary magnet. His demonstrations established that a changing magnetic field produces an electric field; this relation was modelled mathematically by <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> as <a href="/wiki/Faraday%27s_law_of_induction" title="Faraday's law of induction">Faraday's law</a>, which subsequently became one of the four <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell's equations">Maxwell equations</a>, and which have in turn evolved into the generalization known today as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Field_theory_(physics)" title="Field theory (physics)">field theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pioneers_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pioneers-56">[56]</a></sup> Faraday would later use the principles he had discovered to construct the electric <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_generator#Dynamo" title="Electrical generator">dynamo</a>, the ancestor of modern power generators and the electric motor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_and_Daniell_1849_RGNb10408769_f85.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3498" data-file-width="2555" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Faraday_and_Daniell_1849_RGNb10408769_f85.tif/lossy-page1-170px-Faraday_and_Daniell_1849_RGNb10408769_f85.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Faraday_and_Daniell_1849_RGNb10408769_f85.tif/lossy-page1-255px-Faraday_and_Daniell_1849_RGNb10408769_f85.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Faraday_and_Daniell_1849_RGNb10408769_f85.tif/lossy-page1-340px-Faraday_and_Daniell_1849_RGNb10408769_f85.tif.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_and_Daniell_1849_RGNb10408769_f85.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Faraday (right) and <a href="/wiki/John_Frederic_Daniell" title="John Frederic Daniell">John Daniell</a> (left), founders of electrochemistry.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1832, he completed a series of experiments aimed at investigating the fundamental nature of electricity; Faraday used "<a href="/wiki/Electrostatics" title="Electrostatics">static</a>", <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">batteries</a>, and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioelectromagnetism" title="Bioelectromagnetism">animal electricity</a>" to produce the phenomena of electrostatic attraction, <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">magnetism</a>, etc. He concluded that, contrary to the scientific opinion of the time, the divisions between the various "kinds" of electricity were illusory. Faraday instead proposed that only a single "electricity" exists, and the changing values of quantity and intensity (current and voltage) would produce different groups of phenomena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEUK_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-IEEUK-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Near the end of his career, Faraday proposed that electromagnetic forces extended into the empty space around the conductor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pioneers_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pioneers-56">[56]</a></sup> This idea was rejected by his fellow scientists, and Faraday did not live to see the eventual acceptance of his proposition by the scientific community. Faraday's concept of lines of flux emanating from charged bodies and magnets provided a way to visualize electric and magnetic fields; that conceptual model was crucial for the successful development of the electromechanical devices that dominated engineering and industry for the remainder of the 19th century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diamagnetism">Diamagnetism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Faraday&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Diamagnetism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_photograph_ii.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="460" data-file-width="351" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Faraday_photograph_ii.jpg/170px-Faraday_photograph_ii.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Faraday_photograph_ii.jpg/255px-Faraday_photograph_ii.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Faraday_photograph_ii.jpg/340px-Faraday_photograph_ii.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_photograph_ii.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Faraday holding a type of glass bar he used in 1845 to show magnetism affects light in <a href="/wiki/Dielectric" title="Dielectric">dielectric</a> material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 1845, Faraday discovered that many materials exhibit a weak repulsion from a magnetic field: a phenomenon he termed <a href="/wiki/Diamagnetism" title="Diamagnetism">diamagnetism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Faraday also discovered that the plane of <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a> of linearly polarized light can be rotated by the application of an external magnetic field aligned with the direction in which the light is moving. This is now termed the <a href="/wiki/Faraday_effect" title="Faraday effect">Faraday effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pioneers_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pioneers-56">[56]</a></sup> In Sept 1845 he wrote in his notebook, "I have at last succeeded in <i>illuminating a magnetic curve</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Line_of_force" title="Line of force">line of force</a></i> and in <i>magnetising a <a href="/wiki/Ray_(optics)" title="Ray (optics)">ray of light</a></i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Later on in his life, in 1862, Faraday used a spectroscope to search for a different alteration of light, the change of spectral lines by an applied magnetic field. The equipment available to him was, however, insufficient for a definite determination of spectral change. <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Zeeman" title="Pieter Zeeman">Pieter Zeeman</a> later used an improved apparatus to study the same phenomenon, publishing his results in 1897 and receiving the 1902 Nobel Prize in Physics for his success. In both his 1897 paper<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> and his Nobel acceptance speech,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Zeeman made reference to Faraday's work.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Faraday_cage">Faraday cage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Faraday&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Faraday cage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In his work on static electricity, <a href="/wiki/Faraday%27s_ice_pail_experiment" title="Faraday's ice pail experiment">Faraday's ice pail experiment</a> demonstrated that the charge resided only on the exterior of a charged conductor, and exterior charge had no influence on anything enclosed within a conductor. This is because the exterior charges redistribute such that the interior fields emanating from them cancel one another. This shielding effect is used in what is now known as a <a href="/wiki/Faraday_cage" title="Faraday cage">Faraday cage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pioneers_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pioneers-56">[56]</a></sup> In January 1836, Faraday had put a wooden frame, 12ft square, on four glass supports and added paper walls and wire mesh. He then stepped inside and electrified it. When he stepped out of his electrified cage, Faraday had shown that electricity was a force, not an imponderable fluid as was believed at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Field_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Field-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Royal_Institution_and_public_service">Royal Institution and public service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Faraday&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Royal Institution and public service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FaradayFatherThames.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="584" data-file-width="396" decoding="async" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/FaradayFatherThames.jpg/170px-FaradayFatherThames.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/FaradayFatherThames.jpg/255px-FaradayFatherThames.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/FaradayFatherThames.jpg/340px-FaradayFatherThames.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FaradayFatherThames.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Michael Faraday meets <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Father_Thames" title="Father Thames">Father Thames</a>, from <i><a href="/wiki/Punch_(magazine)" title="Punch (magazine)">Punch</a></i> (21 July 1855)</div></div></div>
<p>Faraday had a long association with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Institution_of_Great_Britain" title="Royal Institution of Great Britain">Royal Institution of Great Britain</a>.  He was appointed Assistant Superintendent of the House of the Royal Institution in 1821.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RIBiography_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-RIBiography-63">[63]</a></sup>  He was elected a <a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">Fellow</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> in 1824.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-7">[7]</a></sup> In 1825, he became Director of the Laboratory of the Royal Institution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RIBiography_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-RIBiography-63">[63]</a></sup>  Six years later, in 1833, Faraday became the first <a href="/wiki/Fullerian_Professor_of_Chemistry" title="Fullerian Professor of Chemistry">Fullerian Professor of Chemistry</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Institution_of_Great_Britain" title="Royal Institution of Great Britain">Royal Institution of Great Britain</a>, a position to which he was appointed for life without the obligation to deliver lectures. His sponsor and mentor was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_%27Mad_Jack%27_Fuller" title="John 'Mad Jack' Fuller">John 'Mad Jack' Fuller</a>, who created the position at the Royal Institution for Faraday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beyond his scientific research into areas such as chemistry, electricity, and magnetism at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution" title="Royal Institution">Royal Institution</a>, Faraday undertook numerous, and often time-consuming, service projects for private enterprise and the British government. This work included investigations of explosions in coal mines, being an <a href="/wiki/Expert_witness" title="Expert witness">expert witness</a> in court, and along with two engineers from <a href="/wiki/Chance_Brothers" title="Chance Brothers">Chance Brothers</a> c.1853, the preparation of high-quality optical glass, which was required by Chance for its lighthouses. In 1846, together with <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lyell" title="Charles Lyell">Charles Lyell</a>, he produced a lengthy and detailed report on a serious <a href="/wiki/Explosion" title="Explosion">explosion</a> in the colliery at <a href="/wiki/Haswell,_County_Durham" title="Haswell, County Durham">Haswell, County Durham</a>, which killed 95 miners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-“Explosions”_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-“Explosions”-65">[65]</a></sup> Their report was a meticulous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forensic_investigation" title="Forensic investigation">forensic investigation</a> and indicated that <a href="/wiki/Coal_dust" title="Coal dust">coal dust</a> contributed to the severity of the explosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-“Explosions”_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-“Explosions”-65">[65]</a></sup> The first-time explosions had been linked to dust, Faraday gave a demonstration during a lecture on how ventilation could prevent it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-“Explosions”_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-“Explosions”-65">[65]</a></sup> The report should have warned coal owners of the hazard of coal dust explosions, but the risk was ignored for over 60 years until the 1913 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Senghenydd_Colliery_Disaster" title="Senghenydd Colliery Disaster">Senghenydd Colliery Disaster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-“Explosions”_65-3"><a href="#cite_note-“Explosions”-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lighthouse_lantern_room_with_Fresnel_lens.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="892" data-file-width="642" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Lighthouse_lantern_room_with_Fresnel_lens.png/170px-Lighthouse_lantern_room_with_Fresnel_lens.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Lighthouse_lantern_room_with_Fresnel_lens.png/255px-Lighthouse_lantern_room_with_Fresnel_lens.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Lighthouse_lantern_room_with_Fresnel_lens.png/340px-Lighthouse_lantern_room_with_Fresnel_lens.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lighthouse_lantern_room_with_Fresnel_lens.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lighthouse lantern room from mid-1800s</div></div></div>
<p>As a respected scientist in a nation with strong maritime interests, Faraday spent extensive amounts of time on projects such as the construction and operation of <a href="/wiki/Lighthouse" title="Lighthouse">lighthouses</a> and protecting the bottoms of ships from <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a>. His workshop still stands at <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Buoy_Wharf" title="Trinity Buoy Wharf">Trinity Buoy Wharf</a> above the Chain and Buoy Store, next to London's only lighthouse where he carried out the first experiments in electric lighting for lighthouses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Faraday was also active in what would now be called <a href="/wiki/Environmental_science" title="Environmental science">environmental science</a>, or engineering. He investigated industrial pollution at <a href="/wiki/Swansea" title="Swansea">Swansea</a> and was consulted on air pollution at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mint" title="Royal Mint">Royal Mint</a>. In July 1855, Faraday wrote a letter to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> on the subject of the foul condition of the <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">River Thames</a>, which resulted in an often-reprinted cartoon in <i><a href="/wiki/Punch_(magazine)" title="Punch (magazine)">Punch</a></i>. (See also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Great_Stink" title="The Great Stink">The Great Stink</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_apparatus_for_ideomotor_effect_on_table_turning.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="630" data-file-width="687" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Faraday_apparatus_for_ideomotor_effect_on_table_turning.png/220px-Faraday_apparatus_for_ideomotor_effect_on_table_turning.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Faraday_apparatus_for_ideomotor_effect_on_table_turning.png/330px-Faraday_apparatus_for_ideomotor_effect_on_table_turning.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Faraday_apparatus_for_ideomotor_effect_on_table_turning.png/440px-Faraday_apparatus_for_ideomotor_effect_on_table_turning.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_apparatus_for_ideomotor_effect_on_table_turning.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Faraday's apparatus for experimental demonstration of <a href="/wiki/Ideomotor_phenomenon" title="Ideomotor phenomenon">ideomotor effect</a> on table-turning</div></div></div>
<p>Faraday assisted with the planning and judging of exhibits for the <a href="/wiki/Great_Exhibition" title="Great Exhibition">Great Exhibition</a> of 1851 in London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> He also advised the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Gallery,_London" title="National Gallery, London">National Gallery</a> on the cleaning and protection of its art collection, and served on the National Gallery Site Commission in 1857.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Education was another of Faraday's areas of service; he lectured on the topic in 1854 at the Royal Institution,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> and in 1862 he appeared before a Public Schools Commission to give his views on education in Great Britain. Faraday also weighed in negatively on the public's fascination with <a href="/wiki/Table-turning" title="Table-turning">table-turning</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesmerism" title="Mesmerism">mesmerism</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seance" title="Seance">seances</a>, and in so doing chastised both the public and the nation's educational system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_Michael_Christmas_lecture.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="761" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Faraday_Michael_Christmas_lecture.jpg/220px-Faraday_Michael_Christmas_lecture.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Faraday_Michael_Christmas_lecture.jpg/330px-Faraday_Michael_Christmas_lecture.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Faraday_Michael_Christmas_lecture.jpg/440px-Faraday_Michael_Christmas_lecture.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_Michael_Christmas_lecture.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Faraday delivering a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution_Christmas_Lectures" title="Royal Institution Christmas Lectures">Christmas Lecture</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution" title="Royal Institution">Royal Institution</a> in 1856.</div></div></div>
<p>Before his famous Christmas lectures, Faraday delivered chemistry lectures for the City Philosophical Society from 1816 to 1818 in order to refine the quality of his lectures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Between 1827 and 1860 at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution" title="Royal Institution">Royal Institution</a> in London, Faraday gave a series of nineteen <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution_Christmas_Lectures" title="Royal Institution Christmas Lectures">Christmas lectures</a> for young people, a series which continues today. The objective of the lectures was to present science to the general public in the hopes of inspiring them and generating revenue for the Royal Institution. They were notable events on the social calendar among London's gentry. Over the course of several letters to his close friend Benjamin Abbott, Faraday outlined his recommendations on the art of lecturing, writing "a flame should be lighted at the commencement and kept alive with unremitting splendour to the end".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> His lectures were joyful and juvenile, he delighted in filling soap bubbles with various gasses (in order to determine whether or not they are magnetic), but the lectures were also deeply philosophical. In his lectures he urged his audiences to consider the mechanics of his experiments: "you know very well that ice floats upon water ... Why does the ice float? Think of that, and philosophise".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The subjects in his lectures consisted of Chemistry and Electricity, and included: 1841: <i>The Rudiments of Chemistry</i>, 1843: <i>First Principles of Electricity</i>, 1848: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chemical_History_of_a_Candle" title="The Chemical History of a Candle">The Chemical History of a Candle</a></i>, 1851: <i>Attractive Forces</i>, 1853: <i>Voltaic Electricity</i>, 1854: <i>The Chemistry of Combustion</i>, 1855: <i>The Distinctive Properties of the Common Metals</i>, 1857: <i>Static Electricity</i>, 1858: <i>The Metallic Properties</i>, 1859: <i>The Various Forces of Matter and their Relations to Each Other</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemorations">Commemorations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Faraday&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Commemorations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_things_named_after_Michael_Faraday" title="List of things named after Michael Faraday">List of things named after Michael Faraday</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Michael_Faraday,_Savoy_Place.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2034" data-file-width="1525" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Statue_of_Michael_Faraday%2C_Savoy_Place.jpg/170px-Statue_of_Michael_Faraday%2C_Savoy_Place.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Statue_of_Michael_Faraday%2C_Savoy_Place.jpg/255px-Statue_of_Michael_Faraday%2C_Savoy_Place.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Statue_of_Michael_Faraday%2C_Savoy_Place.jpg/340px-Statue_of_Michael_Faraday%2C_Savoy_Place.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Michael_Faraday,_Savoy_Place.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Faraday in <a href="/wiki/Savoy_Place" title="Savoy Place">Savoy Place</a>, London. Sculptor <a href="/wiki/John_Henry_Foley" title="John Henry Foley">John Henry Foley</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>A statue of Faraday stands in <a href="/wiki/Savoy_Place" title="Savoy Place">Savoy Place</a>, London, outside the <a href="/wiki/Institution_of_Engineering_and_Technology" title="Institution of Engineering and Technology">Institution of Engineering and Technology</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday_Memorial" title="Michael Faraday Memorial">Michael Faraday Memorial</a>, designed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brutalist" title="Brutalist">brutalist</a> architect <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Gordon" title="Rodney Gordon">Rodney Gordon</a> and completed in 1961, is at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elephant_%26_Castle" title="Elephant &amp; Castle">Elephant &amp; Castle</a> gyratory system, near Faraday's birthplace at <a href="/wiki/Newington_Butts" title="Newington Butts">Newington Butts</a>, London. Faraday School is located on <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Buoy_Wharf" title="Trinity Buoy Wharf">Trinity Buoy Wharf</a> where his workshop still stands above the Chain and Buoy Store, next to London's only lighthouse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Faraday Gardens is a small park in <a href="/wiki/Walworth" title="Walworth">Walworth</a>, London, not far from his birthplace at Newington Butts. It lies within the local council ward of Faraday in the <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Southwark" title="London Borough of Southwark">London Borough of Southwark</a>. Michael Faraday Primary school is situated on the <a href="/wiki/Aylesbury_Estate" title="Aylesbury Estate">Aylesbury Estate</a> in <a href="/wiki/Walworth" title="Walworth">Walworth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>A building at <a href="/wiki/London_South_Bank_University" title="London South Bank University">London South Bank University</a>, which houses the institute's electrical engineering departments is named the Faraday Wing, due to its proximity to Faraday's birthplace in <a href="/wiki/Newington_Butts" title="Newington Butts">Newington Butts</a>. A hall at <a href="/wiki/Loughborough_University" title="Loughborough University">Loughborough University</a> was named after Faraday in 1960. Near the entrance to its dining hall is a bronze casting, which depicts the symbol of an electrical <a href="/wiki/Transformer" title="Transformer">transformer</a>, and inside there hangs a portrait, both in Faraday's honour. An eight-story building at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a>'s science &amp; engineering campus is named for Faraday, as is a recently built hall of accommodation at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brunel_University" title="Brunel University">Brunel University</a>, the main engineering building at <a href="/wiki/Swansea_University" title="Swansea University">Swansea University</a>, and the instructional and experimental physics building at <a href="/wiki/Northern_Illinois_University" title="Northern Illinois University">Northern Illinois University</a>. The former UK Faraday Station in <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> was named after him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:27%; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Without such freedom there would have been no <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>, no <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Goethe</a>, no <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton</a>, no Faraday, no <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Pasteur</a> and no <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lister" title="Joseph Lister">Lister</a>.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">—<a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>'s speech on intellectual freedom at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Albert_Hall" title="Royal Albert Hall">Royal Albert Hall</a>, London having fled Nazi Germany, 3 October 1933.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Streets named for Faraday can be found in many British cities (e.g., London, <a href="/wiki/Fife" title="Fife">Fife</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swindon" title="Swindon">Swindon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basingstoke" title="Basingstoke">Basingstoke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nottingham" title="Nottingham">Nottingham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whitby" title="Whitby">Whitby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kirkby" title="Kirkby">Kirkby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crawley" title="Crawley">Crawley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newbury,_Berkshire" title="Newbury, Berkshire">Newbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swansea" title="Swansea">Swansea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aylesbury" title="Aylesbury">Aylesbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stevenage" title="Stevenage">Stevenage</a>) as well as in France (Paris), Germany (<a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>-<a href="/wiki/Dahlem_(Berlin)" title="Dahlem (Berlin)">Dahlem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hermsdorf,_Thuringia" title="Hermsdorf, Thuringia">Hermsdorf</a>), Canada (<a href="/wiki/Quebec_City" title="Quebec City">Quebec City</a>, Quebec; <a href="/wiki/Deep_River,_Ontario" title="Deep River, Ontario">Deep River</a>, Ontario; Ottawa, Ontario), the United States (<a href="/wiki/Reston,_Virginia" title="Reston, Virginia">Reston</a>, Virginia), and New Zealand (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawke%27s_Bay_Region" title="Hawke's Bay Region">Hawke's Bay</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MICHAEL_FARADAY_MAN_OF_SCIENCE._APPRENTICE_HERE._B.1791D.1867.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/MICHAEL_FARADAY_MAN_OF_SCIENCE._APPRENTICE_HERE._B.1791D.1867.jpg/170px-MICHAEL_FARADAY_MAN_OF_SCIENCE._APPRENTICE_HERE._B.1791D.1867.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/MICHAEL_FARADAY_MAN_OF_SCIENCE._APPRENTICE_HERE._B.1791D.1867.jpg/255px-MICHAEL_FARADAY_MAN_OF_SCIENCE._APPRENTICE_HERE._B.1791D.1867.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/MICHAEL_FARADAY_MAN_OF_SCIENCE._APPRENTICE_HERE._B.1791D.1867.jpg/340px-MICHAEL_FARADAY_MAN_OF_SCIENCE._APPRENTICE_HERE._B.1791D.1867.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MICHAEL_FARADAY_MAN_OF_SCIENCE._APPRENTICE_HERE._B.1791D.1867.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plaque erected in 1876 by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Arts" title="Royal Society of Arts">Royal Society of Arts</a> in Marylebone, London</div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Arts" title="Royal Society of Arts">Royal Society of Arts</a> <a href="/wiki/Blue_plaque" title="Blue plaque">blue plaque</a>, unveiled in 1876, commemorates Faraday at 48 Blandford Street in London's Marylebone district.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EngHet_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-EngHet-84">[84]</a></sup> From 1991 until 2001, Faraday's picture featured on the reverse of Series E £20 <a href="/wiki/Banknotes_of_the_pound_sterling" title="Banknotes of the pound sterling">banknotes</a> issued by the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England_note_issues" title="Bank of England note issues">Bank of England</a>. He was portrayed conducting a lecture at the Royal Institution with the magneto-electric spark apparatus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bankofengland_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-bankofengland-85">[85]</a></sup> In 2002, Faraday was ranked number 22 in the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>'s list of the <a href="/wiki/100_Greatest_Britons" title="100 Greatest Britons">100 Greatest Britons</a> following a UK-wide vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Faraday has been commemorated on postage stamps issued by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail" title="Royal Mail">Royal Mail</a>. In 1991, as a pioneer of electricity he featured in their <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_commemorative_stamps_1990%E2%80%931999" title="Great Britain commemorative stamps 1990–1999">Scientific Achievements</a> issue along with pioneers in three other fields (<a href="/wiki/Charles_Babbage" title="Charles Babbage">Charles Babbage</a> (computing), <a href="/wiki/Frank_Whittle" title="Frank Whittle">Frank Whittle</a> (jet engine) and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Watson-Watt" title="Robert Watson-Watt">Robert Watson-Watt</a> (radar)).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> In 1999, under the title "Faraday's Electricity", he featured in their <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_commemorative_stamps_1990%E2%80%931999" title="Great Britain commemorative stamps 1990–1999">World Changers</a> issue along with <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Jenner" title="Edward Jenner">Edward Jenner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Alan Turing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Faraday_Institute_for_Science_and_Religion" title="Faraday Institute for Science and Religion">Faraday Institute for Science and Religion</a> derives its name from the scientist, who saw his faith as integral to his scientific research. The logo of the institute is also based on Faraday's discoveries. It was created in 2006 by a $2,000,000 <a href="/wiki/Grant_(money)" title="Grant (money)">grant</a> from the <a href="/wiki/John_Templeton_Foundation" title="John Templeton Foundation">John Templeton Foundation</a> to carry out academic research, to foster understanding of the interaction between science and religion, and to engage public understanding in both these subject areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Faraday Institution, an independent energy storage research institute established in 2017, also derives its name from Michael Faraday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-“Institution”_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-“Institution”-91">[91]</a></sup> The organisation serves as the UK's primary research programme to advance battery science and technology, education, public engagement and market research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-“Institution”_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-“Institution”-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Faraday's life and contributions to electromagnetics was the principal topic of the tenth episode, titled "<a href="/wiki/The_Electric_Boy" title="The Electric Boy">The Electric Boy</a>", of the 2014 American science documentary series, <i><a href="/wiki/Cosmos:_A_Spacetime_Odyssey" title="Cosmos: A Spacetime Odyssey">Cosmos: A Spacetime Odyssey</a></i>, which was broadcast on <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">Fox</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Channel" title="National Geographic Channel">National Geographic Channel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a>, the literary giant who was also the grandson of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T._H._Huxley" title="T. H. Huxley">T. H. Huxley</a>, the grandnephew of <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Arnold" title="Matthew Arnold">Matthew Arnold</a>, the brother of <a href="/wiki/Julian_Huxley" title="Julian Huxley">Julian Huxley</a>, and the half-brother of <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Huxley" title="Andrew Huxley">Andrew Huxley</a>, was well-versed in science. He wrote about Faraday in an essay entitled, <i>A Night in Pietramala</i>: “He is always the natural philosopher. To discover truth is his sole aim and interest…even if I could be Shakespeare, I think I should still choose to be Faraday.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Calling Faraday her "hero", in a speech to the Royal Society, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> declared: “The value of his work must be higher than the capitalisation of all the shares on the Stock Exchange!”. She borrowed his bust from the Royal Institution and had it placed in the hall of <a href="/wiki/10_Downing_Street" title="10 Downing Street">10 Downing Street</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Field_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Field-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Awards_named_in_Faraday.27s_honour"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_named_in_Faraday's_honour">Awards named in Faraday's honour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Faraday&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Awards named in Faraday's honour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In honor and remembrance of his great scientific contributions, several institutions have created prizes and awards in his name. This include:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Institution_of_Engineering_and_Technology" title="Institution of Engineering and Technology">IET</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faraday_Medal" title="Faraday Medal">Faraday Medal</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_London_Michael_Faraday_Prize" title="Royal Society of London Michael Faraday Prize">Royal Society of London Michael Faraday Prize</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Institute of Physics <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday_Medal_and_Prize" title="Michael Faraday Medal and Prize">Michael Faraday Medal and Prize</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Royal Society of Chemistry Faraday Lectureship Prize<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gallery">Gallery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Faraday&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Gallery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:34.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M_Faraday_Lab_H_Moore.jpg" title="Michael Faraday in his laboratory, c. 1850s."><img alt="Michael Faraday in his laboratory, c. 1850s." data-file-height="917" data-file-width="1361" decoding="async" height="81" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/M_Faraday_Lab_H_Moore.jpg/120px-M_Faraday_Lab_H_Moore.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/M_Faraday_Lab_H_Moore.jpg/180px-M_Faraday_Lab_H_Moore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/M_Faraday_Lab_H_Moore.jpg/240px-M_Faraday_Lab_H_Moore.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Michael Faraday in his laboratory, c. 1850s.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">

By Michael Faraday</th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content plainlist">
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="//ftl.toolforge.org/cgi-bin/ftl?at=viaf&amp;au=38158158&amp;library=OLBP">Online books</a></li>

</tr></tbody></table>
<p>Faraday's books, with the exception of <i>Chemical Manipulation</i>, were collections of scientific papers or transcriptions of lectures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>  Since his death, Faraday's diary has been published, as have several large volumes of his letters and Faraday's journal from his travels with Davy in 1813–1815.
</p>
